Another way of making a poultice for dislocated joints and bones, which relieves pain in those areas and heals fissures and fractures: take clean wool, soak it in vinegar and olive oil, and place it on the affected area.
But in this poultice, there is not the power of restoration, it only heals abscesses and draws out and eliminates pain properly.
Another way of making a poultice that heals broken bones: take leaves from a wild fig tree and poppy plant, crush them all and make a poultice with them.
Another way of making a quick-acting poultice to heal bone fractures and promote resolution of abscess residue: take equal parts of marshmallow root, chamomile, violet flowers, flour, all in equal quantities. Let all the ingredients be crushed and mixed with vinegar, if the member is not damaged.
But if it is damaged, then use a poultice made with crushed barley water or plain water and apply it.
Another way of making a stronger poultice for the resolution of abscesses, when a severe abscess occurs and requires bone restoration: take equal parts of marshmallow root, flax seeds, wild oleander, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, and chamomile. Let all the ingredients be crushed and mixed with pine resin or plain water or vinegar, depending on the affected member's condition and color.
However, Hippocrates says in his book that a poultice should not be placed on the broken member for its restoration, but rather a cerate made from wax and oil without any other ingredients; he also recommends that it be of medium consistency between thick and thin.
However, Galen advises that poultices should be placed on the broken member for its restoration, and the causes of dryness with a small amount of heat should be similar to myrrh, resin, and substances like them from those we have recommended.
Another way of making a poultice for injury and pain: take equal parts of crushed goat's milk cheese, wax from human hair or bird feathers, marshmallow root, and salt; crush, grind, and mix them to make the poultice.
However, the amount of poultice that remains attached should be sufficient to keep the dressing in place, and it should not be loosened for many days if there is no pain, inflammation, or broken bone out of its location. And if pain, swelling, or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should consider loosening or removing the poultice immediately and removing it from the area.
And take a piece of linen and dampen it with seawater, then use that to wash the area where there is swelling and inflammation until the swelling subsides and pain is alleviated; let the member rest for an hour; then place clean wool soaked in vinegar or olive oil on the affected area, and wrap it with a bandage overnight until you are certain that the abscess has been drawn out and the inflammation has subsided, and the pain has been relieved.
And repeat that gentle stretching and apply a thin poultice without applying too much pressure from your initial bandaging, and continue to gently stretch the affected area until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess and redness and pain and inflammation and all the symptoms have completely healed, and you no longer need poultices or bandages, then repeat the same treatment as you did at the beginning in equal amounts.
And if none of the symptoms we mentioned occur in the member, then you should not remove the poultice for three or four or five or seven days. And sometimes everything is left alone for twenty days, depending on the loosening of the member, as we said before, until the fracture heals and begins to form new tissue over it.
And then adjust the bandage so that it is tighter than your initial bandaging and also take into account the patient's nutritional needs and any other factors that may affect their recovery.
However, if you see that the site of the fracture has become dry and shriveled more than necessary, then you should know that nutrition is not reaching it; in this case, soak it with mild salt water each time you change the dressing, for three days. Also loosen the bandage slightly to allow nutrients to reach the affected area and promote faster healing.
However, foolish restorers of bone fractures sometimes cause further damage when the bone does not heal properly at first, and instead heal in a crooked manner. This is a mistake in their procedure, and they should be fearful of it. If they had acted correctly from the beginning, the ancients would have approved of their work.
And I have not seen anyone among them do this, which is more reasonable that they should not do it.
Chapter Two: On Fractures That Occur in the Head.
There are many types of fractures and their shapes vary, as do the causes, for some fractures are caused by blows from weapons that penetrate the skull, entering between the bone and skin, like a stick penetrating through it, and this type is called "ascial." Or a weapon cuts into the bone but does not penetrate deeply, only cutting through the flesh, in which case it is called "levatio absoluta."
And the severity of these two types of fractures can be great or small, and there is also the type of fracture that is caused by friction or scraping against stone or similar objects.
And this type of fracture can be penetrating, reaching the periosteum, which is under the bone, or it can occur on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two types of fractures can be great or small.
And there is also the type of fracture that occurs in the fine hairs of bone tissue and is therefore called "fractura capillar."
And there is also the type of fracture that is caused by blows or strikes from stones and similar objects, which causes a piece of bone to be pushed into deeper tissue layers, like what happens when roof tiles are broken and fragments penetrate inward.
And this is usually done in bodies with hot bones, such as the heads of children.
And all these types of fractures and dislocations are like separate stars. And I will come with remedies for all of this in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by detection on them and by investigation of their symptoms and examination of the rotten flesh that is on them.
And the type of fracture known as "capillary" is recognized by this: there is exposed bone that appears clean and has a black mark on it where the fracture is.
However, in the treatment of fractures, one must first consider the symptoms of the patient, and then, if one sees through these symptoms that it clearly indicates fear (such as vomiting from anger, dilation, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, swelling of the hands and feet with pain in them, and similar symptoms in more severe cases), one should not expect healing.
And if you do not see unusual symptoms, then you can hope for a cure and begin the treatment.
This is because if the patient comes to you in the beginning of the illness, as it is during the winter months, then it is necessary to wait for 14 days and observe the condition of the bone before beginning treatment.
And if it is during the summer months, then it is necessary to wait 7 days before cutting into the bone, allowing for any tissue damage or decay below the skin (panniculus) to heal first and for any alarming symptoms to appear.
And if the fracture has reached the panniculus covering the brain and is broken into small pieces and shattered, then it is necessary to cut into the damaged, crushed bone as I will instruct you to do.
And you must examine the head of the patient and uncover the bone as is customary and according to your abilities based on the appearance of the injury, doing so gently with the patient's comfort in mind.
And if you are unable to identify the bone or observe signs of inflammation, such as swelling or pus, then it is necessary to take action by applying wet bandages soaked in vinegar and rose oil to the wound to calm the infection, allowing time for the patient to recover before performing surgery on the bone.
And this is to be done in one of two ways: methods of operation.
And the first method is to cut into the bone with a straight-edged scalpel, and this is how it should be done: first, administer one scalpel that is slightly narrower in width, and then use another scalpel, slightly wider than the first, like so.
And then apply a wider scalpel than the second one.
And it is necessary to have a variety of scalpels with different widths, some wider than others and some narrower, and these should be sharpened on the ends so as to allow for precise cuts.
And make use of them as needed for percussion on the scalpel, making sure not to damage the skull or injure it further.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary to drill into its circumference before using the scalpels with their handles (known as non-penetrating trephines).
And these are called non-penetrating trephines because they do not reach the base of the skull (the inner table) and instead have a rounded tip or edge that prevents them from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is advisable to have many trephines available, each suited for the thickness of different bones, so that you always have one on hand with a sharp tip proportionate to the size of the skull's circumference.
And this is the method for using three types of trephines: to drill into the circumference of a broken bone, place the trephine on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated, then remove the trephine and place it in another location. Repeat this process, leaving enough space between each hole for the width of a ray or thereabouts.
And then use the scalpels to cut between each hole in the bone, making your incisions as fine as possible for ease of removal, either with your hand or through some other means using tools you have prepared for this purpose, such as tweezers and delicate forceps.
And it is essential that you exercise extreme caution to ensure that the scalpel does not come into contact with any part of the brain.
And once you have drilled around the bone and separated it from the brain if it is attached, and extracted it, then it is necessary to smooth and shape the surface of the bone with another tool similar to a scalpel, but sharper and more delicate than the others.
And if any small, fragmented pieces of bone remain, then remove them with ease using the tools at your disposal, and then care for the wound with liniments and ointments as I have mentioned before.
And the other method of operation is less invasive and poses less risk, known as Galen's technique, which involves cutting along the length of the bone where the fracture is most severe and visible. The process for doing this is as follows: in the beginning, carefully uncover the area around the fractured bone until you reach the point where the break is most pronounced, then gently insert the end of this scalpel (whose shape is depicted here) beneath the surface of the bone.
And the lens-shaped portion of the scalpel should be blunt and not cut through anything, while the pointed end should extend two finger widths beyond the handle (as you can see), with the lens-shaped portion resting against the brain and the sharp edge cutting into the bone.
And then strike the scalpel gently with a small mallet until it is fully inserted into the bone in a smooth and easy motion, ensuring that the membrane (panniculus) remains unharmed and not damaged in any way. And if the patient is the most foolish and ignorant of all people, even if they are asleep, then do this without hesitation or fear.
And if there are any attached pieces of the panniculus membrane still adhered to the bone in certain areas, use the round tip of the scalpel to gently cut through them and release them without causing harm or risk.
And if the fracture does not penetrate into the membrane (panniculus) and instead only affects the surface of the bone, leaving behind jagged edges or fragments, then it is necessary to smooth out these rough areas and remove the small fragments using fine razors that you have prepared in advance. These razors should come in various sizes so that they can be used effectively in any situation and with precision based on your assessment of the broken bone and its shape.
And it is essential that you begin by using the largest of your razors to shape the bone, and then gradually move on to finer ones as needed, continuing this process until you have used the finest instruments available to complete the procedure.
And for smaller, less severe capillary and fine fractures, provide care based on your assessment of the necessary treatment.
And it is because not everyone who studies this art has achieved mastery over all types of fractures. And we have demonstrated this in cases of major fractures.
And if the membrane (panniculus) retracts when you remove the bone fragments, then take a piece of it equivalent to the size of the wound and soak it in rose oil before applying it to the opening. Then place another folded or tripled cloth soaked in vinegar over this first layer, being as gentle as possible so as not to damage the brain. Finally, apply a broad bandage but do not pull it too tight, only enough to cover the wound.
And leave him to this treatment for one day or for two until you are sure of the abscess formation; and then release it and administer medicines which are excessively dry, such as linseed and flour of erbs, and powder of aloes and licorice, and similar to these.
And make a powder of these medicines which should be pulverized over the wound, as they are dry; and generally, administer all medicine with the property that it repels and does not bite.
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean; and not be contaminated with pus or other things; nor allow any virulence to accumulate in it; for when it adheres to the dura mater of the brain, it corrupts it and then harms the patient, causing a malignant evil.
And it occurs in the head, as when the bone is purified and mostly when the treatment of it is neglected, and there is blackness on the surface; and therefore consider at that time if such symptoms occur in the patient, know that he is in great danger.
And if it is not blackness but from the medicine that is placed on top, and it is due to the power of that medicine that causes such blackness, therefore you should take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil. And soak linen in it and place the cloth on the head; and treat it with spices of healing until it is healed.
Chapter 3
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not broken if there are two pieces together at a higher level, or one of those in some places, because there are two bones in it, and the lower part is cartilaginous and not broken, but rather abrasion, curvature, and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you should insert the little finger into its nostril and press the fracture from inside with your index finger, and from outside with the thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do so with ease, and take care that you do not cause pain to the patient during your operation.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the little finger cannot reach it, then you should use a thin rod to reduce it.
And if there are fractures in two parts, then you should do the same.
And strive for the restoration of it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then wait for the seventh or tenth day after the abscess formation.
And insert into the tip of the nose a piece of linen cloth if the fracture is in one part, or place two cotton swabs if the fracture is in two parts, and make sure that their size is such that they fill the nostril.
Some of the ancient restorers say that the swabs should be soaked in bread crumbs and changed every day, but I do not see it; it is necessary that the piece of cloth is soaked in egg white mixed with powdered alum; and then leave one swab inside until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes insert pieces of linen instead of swabs, in order to avoid the accumulation of pus that is wrapped in a cloth of lion; and this should be done when the fracture of the nose is more severe and the patient cannot breathe through their nose.
And this method is not necessary for you; and do as you please, whether it be inserting strips of linen or using swabs.
And if another abscess forms, apply a compress to the nose with turpentine or wax infused with vinegar and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if there is no abscess formation, then you should apply a compress to it from outside with flour of cumin and powdered aloes mixed with egg white; and place a lion's skin over it, and do not bind the nose with any cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or simply comminuted, then you should cut open above them and extract those with instruments that suit them; and close the incision, and heal it with what is suitable for embedding and consolidating, and with appropriate compresses.
And if there is an infection inside the nose, then you should treat it with swabs and insert lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4
On healing a fractured jawbone.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by infection, then look to see if the fracture is only displaced outwardly, and not divided into two parts but curved inwardly; in this case, its identification is easy.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, insert your right index finger; and gently push the swelling of the fracture inward with that finger, and with your other hand from outside the bone, guide it back into place with gentle pressure.
And if the jawbone fracture has already separated into two parts, then you should administer extension to both parts according to rectitude until it is possible to align them.
And if there is dislocation of teeth due to the fracture, then strengthen those that you hope will remain with wire made of gold or silver or lead; and apply a compress of wax on the broken jawbone, and place a double layer of cloth over it, and then place a thick piece of wood or a wooden splint, well-made, or a sole of a shoe the length of the jawbone over that; and bind it above with wire as you have prepared for tying it, according to what is appropriate for its application, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to rest and remain calm, and feed them soft foods like lion's meat.
And if you estimate that the shape has been altered in some way, then you should strive to release and correct it on the third day; and rectify what has been altered and apply a compress of powdered alum or cumin flour mixed with egg white over it, once you have removed the wax from it, and place wool from a lion over the compress.
And once the compress is attached and properly aligned with the bone, do not remove it until the fracture has healed and strengthened, because many times this type of fracture heals within three weeks.
And if another abscess forms, then use what we have mentioned many times in the treatment of it until it is healed, and let it drain.
And if the fracture is accompanied by infection, then consider whether it is a pus-filled abscess or cellulitis; in this case, you should be careful to remove those cellulitic areas with appropriate instruments during its removal.
And if the openings of the infection are narrow, then you should widen them with a fleam-botomy according to your needs.
And when you have removed those cellulitic areas and there is no more drainage from them, then close the openings of the infection if they are wide; and if not, apply one of the compresses that suits it and cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Chapter 5
On healing a fractured furcula (wishbone) when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken at the front part near the muscle, and this fracture is made in one of three ways.
The clavicle can be broken in three ways: either it is broken, or it is separated into two parts without injuring the blood vessels; this type of fracture is easy to heal. Alternatively, the break can damage the blood vessels, making it difficult to heal. Lastly, the clavicle can be shattered into multiple pieces, which is also difficult to heal.
And the operation for this type of fracture, when it is shattered, is to have two assistants present, so that one can hold the broken clavicle in place with assistance and the other can stretch the neck towards the affected area.
Then align the fracture with the fingers, ensuring that it is shaped properly and neither protruding nor concave.
And if more extension is needed, then place a roll of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit, adjusting its size according to your needs; extend and raise the clavicle, and press down on the roll with your hand while aligning the fracture as necessary.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle to align it with the rest, because it has become attached to the lung, then have the patient recline on their neck and place a medium-sized cushion under the affected muscle.
And the assistant should press on the muscle to pull it towards the aligned clavicle until the bone is raised out of the lung and then you can adjust and align the fracture with your fingers.
But if you feel blood vessels in the clavicle and they seem damaged, then make an incision over them and carefully remove the damaged vessels from within.
And if it is trapped in the bone, then you should use your ingenuity to make an incision in it with one of the scalpels that you have prepared for this purpose, since you have placed a tool underneath the clavicle which can be used as a retractor; and this tool is made of wood or metal, with the following shape: [The text here describes the shape of a U-shaped retractor, which would be used to hold back the edges of the incision.]
And it is similar to a spoon, with this being the shape of its concavity; and its width should be according to what you need for it, depending on the size of the bone or its smallness.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible for you during the operation; it should have two ends, one wide and the other narrow.
And if the wound caused by the incision made when you removed the damaged vessel is wide and there is a risk of infection or abscess, then you should stitch together the edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the wound is small or you are worried about infection, then you should fill the wound with cloth and powder according to the size of the incision in the wound.
And if an abscess forms, then insert a cloth soaked in vinegar, rose oil, and wine over it.
And overall, make sure that the board does not injure the broken bone; and therefore, you should examine the patient every day, checking if the swelling has softened or if the board has become misaligned, in which case you should adjust it and strengthen the binding.
And place a pillow under the patient's head and place a small cushion under their armpit during sleep, so that with this they can raise the arm on the side of the fracture, elevating the clavicle to the level of the muscle; or bind the arm to the neck.
And do not loosen the binding unless there is a wound or abscess in the area within 12 days.
And change the plaster if you see it becoming dirty, adjusting the binding to fit the stricture and restoring it until the fracture is healed and solidified.
And if it has become strong and comfortable within 28 days, then the healing may be complete in some individuals before this time.
Chapter 6.
Chapter Six: On the healing of shoulder fractures.
The shoulder blade (spatula) is broken at its side, but not separated into pieces; only the edges are broken.
And so when the shoulder blade is broken at its midpoint and not easily visible, then according to the shape of the fracture, prepare for it and reduce it to its natural shape in every way that you can using any means necessary.
Then place on the site powder of flour and albumen from eggs and lion's paw, then place two layers of cloth over it, and then place a thin wooden board the size of the entire shoulder blade or slightly smaller.
But if the board left over some parts of the shoulder blade is concave, then you should align that concavity with lion's paw, and then raise the board to be even.
Then wrap it tightly with a long bandage, with gentle pressure on the board to keep it in place without allowing it to become misaligned.
And examine the binding every day; and whenever it has become loose, rebind it with a new bandage and adjust the board if it has shifted from its position.
And keep the patient on their side with the affected arm raised above the head.
The shoulder blade heals in 20 or 25 days.
And when this number of days has passed, then you should loosen the binding, making sure that the bone is solid and there is no danger of a new fracture occurring.
And if a wound is present in the bone and you place your finger behind it, then make an incision over it and remove any damaged tissue or pus that may have formed there, as we previously discussed for shoulder blade fractures with risk of infection.
Chapter 7.
On the healing of foot fractures.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this type of break is difficult to treat; however, the ends of the bones are more often crushed and splintered. [
When the middle of the foot is broken, it tends to bend inwards and causes severe pain, difficulty walking, coughing, shortness of breath, easy sweating, and vomiting blood; this condition is revealed by a cavity in the broken bone and cannot be ignored or overlooked.
The treatment for this type of foot fracture involves having the patient lie on their back with a cushion placed between their shoulder blades. The muscles around the injured area should be gently massaged and manipulated to help realign the bones, while the ribcage is supported by the hands of two assistants who carefully adjust the positioning of the ribs as needed. This process requires a high level of skill and precision to ensure that the broken bone is restored to its natural shape and alignment.
Then place a plaster and lion's paw over the affected area and put a thin wooden board made of pine or chestnut (or similar) on top, wrapped in cloth. Gently manipulate the broken bone into its correct position without causing further injury, and then wrap it tightly with bandages to hold everything in place. Keep a close eye on the binding at all times, and when it becomes loose, tighten it again.
And if necessary, loosen the binding due to inflammation, irritation, pain, or infection in the area. Remove the plaster and treat the issue with appropriate medical care. Once resolved, replace the plaster and adjust the bandage as needed until the injury has healed completely.
Chapter 8.
On the healing of ribs when they are fractured.
Know that rib fractures only occur in large areas close to the back, and not at their ends near the front, as they are made of cartilage. And the knowledge of this condition is not hidden because it can be detected through examination using fingers.
The healing of rib fractures should be done by aligning the broken bones with the fingers as much as possible, ensuring that they are realigned correctly so that they can heal properly. Additionally, a plaster and wooden board can be used to stabilize the area if necessary.
If the rib fracture is angled towards the inside, then the patient will experience intense pain and puncturing sensations, similar to what one might feel if they had pleurisy. This occurs because the bone presses against the lining of the lungs, causing difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood. Such injuries are difficult to treat.
The Ancients have already invented many ingenious methods for treating this condition.
And according to what they said, it is necessary to feed the patient food that produces flatulence and inflammation, so that the stomach expands and pushes outwards, thus pushing the fracture towards the outside of the body.
However, we avoid this approach to prevent the risk of infection or abscess, unless it is necessary; and if it has already occurred, then help with that.
Some of them said that it is necessary to place the patient on their back and use suction to draw out the air; this may seem reasonable, but there is a concern that the suction could pull excess fluid or matter into the injury site due to its weakness.
And some of them said that it is necessary to cover the area with wool soaked in cauterizing oil, and then place powders between the ribs until they are full. The binding should be wrapped around the patient as snugly as possible. Additionally, the patient's overall care should follow the same approach used for treating pleurisy, including both food and medicines.
And if the illness causes violent constriction which the patient cannot bear, and the bones beneath the belly swell with a lenticular abscess, then we must be cautious of the patient. It is necessary to make an incision in the location, reveal the rib bone that has been broken, and place our instrument that preserves sterility over it. The memory of this action should be preceded, and we should carefully insert the instrument, facilitating its entry, and extract that [abscess].
Then we must adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitches, and treat it with plasters until it heals.
If an abscess occurs internally, you should place and press gently with a cotton ball soaked in rose oil over the area, and treat the patient as needed for the internal abscess.
And have them propped up on a pillow under the leg where they sleep, until it heals.
Chapter 9.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Some remedies for fractures in the bones of the neck, and care must be taken with their friction, similarly with the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will recover or not, consider: for if you see their hands and feet already relaxed, numb, and lifeless, unable to move them, stretch or strengthen them, and when you constrict them with your hands or prick them with a needle, they feel no pain, then you know that they are not recovering. In the best case scenario, this condition is deadly.
But if they react to touch and feel pain from constriction or pricking, then you know that the marrow of the bone is preserved, and the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see similar signs in the cervical vertebrae and want to know whether they will recover or not, then observe their feet.
And if you see that they are already numb and similar symptoms appear in their hands, and when they lie on their back, there is shortness of breath and defecation without their volition, or if they are often propped up on their backs and want to urinate but cannot, then know that it is deadly, and do not waste your time with treatment.
But if none of those symptoms appear, then the cause is better and more lightweight.
And the treatment for this is to apply a compress to the abscess on the compressed vertebra, using rose oil or a small amount of crushed onion; place this compress over it several times a day until the abscess subsides.
And then apply a strengthening and invigorating plaster, and bind it firmly with bandages; also instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, not to sleep on the affected side that causes pain until they are healed.
And if there is friction in the affected bone or some cause that has separated from it, then you must saw through the spine and remove the bone. Then adjust the two lips of the wound, stitching them together if necessary; and treat the area with some healing salve until it heals.
But if the tailbone is broken and it is the end of the spine, then you must insert your left index finger into the rectum and manipulate the fractured bone with your other hand as best as possible using anatomical alignment. Then apply a plaster and splint, if necessary, and bind it firmly.
And if you feel friction between the broken bones, then you must saw through them and remove the fragments, treating the wound with the necessary medical care until it heals.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the spine.
Spinal bones are slow to break, but when they do, the fracture is not limited to their ends, but rather extends into their length and curves inward. This results in pain at the affected area, as well as numbness or paralysis of the limbs.
The restoration of the spine involves extending your hand over the affected area, aligning it with the shape of the fracture.
If the fracture is only at the end of the spine, then you must align it according to its natural shape using appropriate tools until it regains its normal form.
But if the fracture is in the length of the spine or has curved inward, then you must have the patient lie on their stomach while you prepare for the restoration of the fracture. And so when you have aligned the fracture, apply a plaster and place a splint of wood or bone on top of it, securing it firmly with bandages but being careful not to cause further injury or dislodge the splint.
And shape the curve with what is needed to fill it until it is aligned properly and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or side so as not to disturb the alignment.
But if an abscess forms, then you must cease any attempts at realignment until the abscess has been drained and healed. Apply appropriate medical treatment to drain it and wait for it to heal before resuming realignment efforts.
And if the bones are splintered or crushed, then you must treat them.
Chapter 11.
On the restoration of the adductor muscle fracture.
The adductor is the muscle between the thigh and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of the adductor muscle can be done in one of two ways. The first way is to use a flat piece of wood, about half an inch thick, shaped as follows:
And there should be two leather straps at the ends, and it should be suspended from an elevated location.
And the patient should lie on a cushion, and their broken arm should be draped over the wooden piece so that it aligns with the midpoint of the curve.
And it should be hung with some heavy weight or the nurse should lower it gradually. Meanwhile, the doctor should manipulate the fracture with their hands, aligning it properly until it is healed.
The other method is to have the patient lie on their back with their head propped up and their arm suspended by a strap. Then, two assistants should hold the upper and lower parts of the limb, each extending it gently in their direction, while the doctor manipulates the fracture with their hands until it is aligned properly.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then secure the fracture with bandages and have each assistant pull on two ends of the limb, providing more force in the alignment process.
And so if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, then half of the bandage should be placed on the midpoint of the limb and the other half below the fracture towards the thigh.
Similarly, if the fracture is near the thigh, then the bandage should be placed over the fracture and over the thigh.
Align the fracture with ease and without unnecessary effort, and once it is aligned properly and applied correctly, then secure it with bandages, unless an abscess forms.
But if an abscess forms, then leave the bandages loose for seven days and apply a compress of wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil to promote healing, and only then should you reapply the bandages.
The method for securing the bandages is to apply a plaster over the fracture, then wrap an outer layer of clean cloth around it. Then, position the arm in the sling and have the patient place their open hand on their abdomen or hip.
Wrap the cloth around the sling to hold the arm in place and prevent it from moving, unless there is a reason not to do so due to the broken bone.
And then use splints.
And then place the strongest and most rigid splint over the fracture site, with a gap between each splint equal to the width of a finger. The length of the splints should be slightly longer than the length of the fracture, adding three fingers' width on either side.
And then secure the splints with the tightness that I described at the beginning of this chapter.
And ensure that your bandaging over the fracture site is tighter. And as the fracture extends further, use less tension in the bandages.
And so, once you have determined the placement and tension of the splints as I have described during the restoration process of the limb, then proceed accordingly.
And if you are concerned about an abscess forming, then leave the bandages and splints loose for seven days as I have previously described.
And for three days, check the bandages in all parts to ensure that there is no swelling or inflammation, and adjust the tension as necessary so that circulation is not cut off. Also, be sure that food and drink are not restricted from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandages. Make any necessary corrections as previously described.
But if you are confident that there will be no issues, then do not loosen the bandages for three days or more.
And let the patient's head rest on the pillow, and their hands be placed on their stomach.
Place a cushion filled with soft wool under the affected member, and throughout the night or day, observe that the shape of the broken member does not become loose or dissolved, or that the ligaments have not become weakened. If so, correct it with your expertise.
And let the patient's food be served according to the order previously mentioned, so that the food is easily digestible at first but becomes more substantial as digestion progresses.[*]
It is customary for the splint and bandage to be strengthened for 40 days, but then they should be loosened and treated with a bath and ointments that will help correct any issues.
And if the fracture is still unhealed, then do not loosen the ligaments and bones for one or two months.
Chapter 12.
On the restoration of a broken arm.
An arm is composed of two bones which are called "two ulnae" - one is small and located on the side of the thumb, and is called the radius; the other is larger and lies below the smaller one, towards the lower side of the arm, and is called the ulna.
And it is relatively easy to break only the larger bone or only the smaller one, but they are also often broken together at the same time.
And when only the smaller bone is broken near the elbow joint, then its restoration and healing are relatively easy.
But if the larger bone is broken below the elbow joint, then the fracture is severe and healing is difficult.
And the most severe type of break is when both bones are broken at the same time.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller one near the elbow joint, then it is advisable for the physician to perform a simple and easy reduction before applying a cast.
But if the larger bone is broken, then it is necessary to apply a more forceful reduction. [*]
And if both bones are broken at the same time, then it is necessary to apply a much stronger reduction. [*]
And it is necessary to position the hand in such a way that its shape and extension match the reduction and manipulation of the broken bones. This should be done on a cushion with the wrist positioned above all other fingers, except for the thumb, which should be lower than the other fingers. The patient should be lying down on a slanted pillow so that their head is slightly raised. [*]
And there should be a cushion in front of the patient at their eye level or slightly lower, to prevent them from straining or working too hard during the reduction and manipulation.
Then have the assistant extend the patient's arm from the lower side with their hand or bandage, and another assistant should extend it from above in a similar manner. This will provide support and stability to the broken bones during the reduction and manipulation.
And let the physician cast the bone in such a way that it can be reduced as closely to its original shape as possible. [*]
And if there is any displacement in the fracture of the bone, then it is necessary to realign the entire limb in its proper position with your expertise and skill. [*]
And if there is a separate displacement in the fracture that cannot be aligned with the rest of the bone, then it is necessary to perform surgery and remove the displaced section according to the method described earlier. [*]
But if the fracture is accompanied by an infection or abscess, then refer to the previous chapter on this topic for guidance on how to treat it. [*]
And if an abscess or boil forms at the beginning of your treatment, then apply a cloth soaked in a salve made from roasted oil and white wax, with a consistency that is halfway between thickness and thinness. Place this cloth over the boil with gentle pressure until it subsides. Then remove the salve and apply a plaster prepared with powdered chalk and egg white, and place a splint over the fracture that is wider and stronger. [*]
And you should know that there are two types of splints for the arm: one for single-bone fractures and another for double-bone fractures. [*]
And apply strong and firm pressure over the site of the fracture, with more force and vehemence as needed. If the reduction requires greater tension in the areas above or below the fracture, then use a slightly looser bandage as described at the beginning of this chapter.
And let the bandages that are wrapped around the fracture be made from soft and moist cloths, neither hard nor rough. The thread used to sew them should be of medium consistency, neither too fine nor too thick, as previously recommended.
And observe the limb and ligaments for several days. And if some cause arises that requires correction, such as swelling in the limb, then it is necessary to straighten and align the limb with hot water until the swelling subsides.
And allow the limb to rest without constraint until it heals, and then gradually reapply the bandage or splint.
And if the ligaments have become weakened and the bone has begun to heal but is not yet fully aligned, then correct it with your expertise and skill.
And observe whether the nutrients are unable to reach the limb due to excessive constriction from the bandages. If so, then loosen them slightly and allow the limb to rest for several days until the nutrients can flow freely again, and then gradually reapply the bandage or splint with greater firmness.
But if the patient does not experience any of the complications mentioned above, then there is no need to remove the bandages or splint for at least 20 days or so.
And let the patient's arm be suspended by its own power of movement and agitation, allowing them to sleep on their back. [*]
And you should know that this fracture can be healed in 30 or 32 days, or it may heal more quickly in as few as 28 days, depending on the condition and strength of the patient's complexion and constitution.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of a broken finger or toe.
You should know that the fingers of the hand and toenails are prone to bruising but not usually to fractures.
If the hand experiences a fracture or bruising, then it is important for the patient to keep it elevated and in front of them with equal weight distribution, while placing their hand on top of it with the fingers extended.
And let the assistant extend the broken bones while the physician sets them in proper alignment and position.
And then apply a plaster and compress, unless there is an abscess or boil present; in that case, place a splint over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it with linen bandages.
But if the fracture is on the inner side of the palm, then make a cup shape out of cloth and instruct the patient to clench their fist around it. Then secure it with long strips of bandages.
And let the splint be made of wood with linen wrapped around it, to ensure that the wood is applied to the palm, and secure it as needed.
But if the fracture is on the outer side, then place one splint above and another below it in the palm, so that the hand remains open and straight. Then apply a bandage around it, as if wrapping up the hand loosely with string.
If there is a fracture in one of the toes, then if it is the big toe, it should be set as needed and secured to the palm. If desired, a small splint can be placed on it to keep the fracture elevated and prevent deformity.
But if the fracture is in the other toes, such as the middle toe or the index toe or the thumb or the ring finger, then it should be set and bound to the adjacent toe or all of them together, which is better. Or place a small splint on top of it, as previously mentioned with the big toe.
And in your restoration, consider the presence of abscesses or boils and perform any necessary opposing or draining operations if any such causes arise, as previously described.
Chapter 14.
Chapter 14: Treatment of Pelvic Fractures
The bones of the thigh are often found to be broken and it is determined by sensation according to how it is turned in front and above.
The restoration of it is done in such a way that the limb is strengthened over the fracture and another limb is placed below the fracture, and the patient should be laid face down on their belly.
And then the minister extends the entire limb to its full length according to its quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is closer to the knee, then the limb should be placed next to the shin at the end of the kneecap, so that the extension happens above it, and another limb below the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is closer to the shin, then the limb should be placed next to the shin, and the extension should happen at the lower ends.
And then the doctor joins the bones with both his hands until it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined properly; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless pus forms in the member. But if pus forms in it, then you should leave it alone for several days until the abscess has subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done in the following way: it is necessary to wrap a hard bandage over the fracture, fold it two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap the thigh from there until it reaches the knee and the thigh.
And insert a long piece of string between the thigh and the leg near the shin at the lower ends, and bring the ends of the string from the upper part of both the leg and the thigh, and then wrap it around the leg and thigh, covering what is excess from the bandage.
And then place a piece of wood over the thigh at the site of the fracture and place one stick over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the thigh and leg with soft rags, in order to coagulate the bandaging.
And then begin the strong bandaging in the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four layers, and as you move away from the limb at the site of the fracture, your bandaging should be less tight, lighter, and softer.
And then stretch both ends of the string to the lower ends until it reaches the ankle bone of the foot, and tighten both ends of the sticks on the other side so that the limb is not dislocated from its place.
And then leave the bandaging in place, as long as there is no corruption, abscess, or inflammation in the member, and if any of those occur, then be careful to unbind it from that, and correct what happened immediately, as we have said many times before.
But if pus forms in the bone and puts pressure on it, then you must try to join it as best you can; and if not, then operate on it and remove what is possible, and cure it with whatever is necessary until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is strengthened with sticks in such a way that they are adjusted to it, as we have said before in the case of the arm and forearm.
But when the restoration is done in this way, it should not cause the patient to have a limp; and if it heals on its own without the application of the other leg, then it is necessary for that person to always have a limp.
And know that the thigh heals in 40 days or a little more or less, and all of this depends on the diversity of the complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Chapter 15:
Know that a fracture of the kneecap takes a long time to heal, but it often causes significant swelling.
But if it is a fracture, then it is not a complete break but rather a crack or hole in its parts, and this can happen with one or more cracks, and all of it depends on the sensation.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that what has been broken in its parts is joined together with the mentioned methods until it is healed and joined as well as possible, taking into account ease, feasibility, and formation.

And then consider all the dispositions that we have recommended for other fractures, such as pus and similar things, in order to prevent any accidents until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Chapter 16:
Know that there are two bones in the leg, one of which is thick and is called by the name of the leg, and the other is thin and is called shinbone.
And it often happens that either of the two types of fractures occurs in the leg bones, and for this reason, their restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm bones, and the operation is also similar.
And if both leg bones are broken together, then the leg is twisted in all directions.
And if the shin bone is fractured, the leg becomes weakened in the front parts.
And if the thighbone is broken, it causes a swelling below it, and this is one of those injuries that are not minor.
Then administer extension, reduction, and binding of the limbs according to the injury.
However, if the fracture of the leg has many adhesions, then the extension should be less and more gentle, and relieve it with your power.

Then wrap each one in two layers of cloth according to its length, and place one above the leg at the front and the other at the back, and let them be from the heel to the toes.

Then consider the leg every day and study it carefully, and protect it from abscesses, inflammation, or other similar causes; and if any of those occur, then oppose them with what is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the ankle and bones of the foot and toes.
The ankle is rarely broken, but the bones of the foot are sometimes broken, and the toes are seldom broken, and they are not injured except by dislocation in most cases.
And if the bones of the foot are broken and sometimes those bones rise above each other, then let the patient put their foot on the ground prepared as if walking. [continues with the text:
Then place one foot on something raised above those bones, then press and align them until they return to their places.
Then apply a poultice and bandage it, and place a small board under the sole of the foot with two flat ends.

And after three or four days have passed, then you can loosen the bandages, for you will find that the bones are aligned, or broken or dislocated, and the operation in those cases is as you know it.
But if some toes are broken, then restore them and align them as you know in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. And it is important that you do not forget to consider all the causes that I mentioned in other fractures and injuries, and deal with any incident as necessary.

Of the fracture of the thighbone, penis bone, and man's rod.

Then place a pillow under her hips, and when she needs to urinate, remove the cotton with ease and let her urinate, and then replace the cotton as it was before, and return her to her original pillow. Chapter 19.
Chapter 19: On the restoration of bone fractures when wounded.
It is appropriate for one who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large like the thighbone or shinbone, to ensure that they are bled as soon as possible if the conditions for bloodletting indicate so, as we have said before.
And if the wound flows with blood, then it is appropriate to take care in stopping it by powdering over it with yarrow three hundred [times], unless another cause is present.
And then begin restoring the fracture on that same day and do not delay unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess forms, then you should delay the restoration of the fracture for nine days until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day at all, for you will cause harm.
But if the broken bone protrudes beyond the skin and is exposed, then it is appropriate to perform surgery to reduce it and align it with your hands with ease and minimal extension.
But if extension is not prepared and alignment with the tissues is difficult, then you can reduce it with your hands using this instrument, which is made of iron, and its length should be seven or eight fingers wide and its width should correspond to the size of the wound. And for this reason, it is necessary for the doctor to take three or four of these in accordance with the quantity that is needed for treatment in all types of fractures.
And be round, and of moderate size, so that it is not doubled over during the compression in the hour of the operation.
And let it have a sharp point at both ends, with a blunt, thickened section in the middle.
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre voluntatem telarum", which means "small spatula".
Therefore, it is necessary for the sharp end to be placed raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And press it into a groove so that it returns the bone, and let it be glued with some substance.
And therefore make sure to glue the ends of the fracture together, and if the broken end is small and does not take hold of the instrument well, then you must insert the end of that bone into the instrument so that it has the power to do so.
But if you cannot reduce the bone with what we have recommended, then insert it with whatever instruments are necessary, which we have mentioned before; or saw it with one of the saws that is possible for you to use in some way.
And carefully remove any remaining roughness or small shavings from the bone.
And when the bone is reduced and the patient still feels intense pain when it is moved, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position; and therefore do what will bring great relief to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then submerge it in a bath of stiptic black wine, and particularly if it is summertime, and do not place any wax nor other substance that contains oil on the wound, so that no purulence or corruption can occur.
And when your restoration of the bone is complete, then administer styptic agents during this time, and leave the wound uncovered so that you can insert a probe with forceps and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And be sure to exercise all caution so that you do not strain the wound with the fracture, for many foolish doctors have done this and caused their patients to suffer from death, corruption, or severe illness, which is to say, health. And if the ligament is slightly loose and flexible, different from the tightness of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or severe, and you fear that it may be prone to complications that we have mentioned before, and you find pain in the area, then it is not advisable to use styptic agents, but rather apply a bandage of hard cloth instead of styptic agents and strengthen it with those.
And when after one or two days the wound begins to heal and generate pus, then remove the cloth that you had placed over it with wine, and then administer warm compresses, and apply ointments such as those that we use for wounds, such as tetrafarmacon and similar things.
And it is advisable for you to loosen the ligament and examine the wound every day in the morning and evening until it has healed and solidified, and it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus can easily flow from it to the surrounding areas.
If several days have passed and the wound has not healed or separated from it the pus, then know that this is a symptom of stagnation, for which reason you must inquire about the wound with probing. And of those stagnations that are separate and external, those that are not separated and putrefy the limb and cause pain, be sure to incise those if it is possible for you to do so.
And if there comes to the wound an abscess or ulcer, that is to say, stymenia, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary to apply one of those things that are appropriate for its cure, which is described in its chapter.
And in that matter where it is necessary for my words to remain and you present your own opinion: when the large bone breaks, protrudes over the limb like the bone of the thigh and the shinbone and similar bones of the large limbs, and do not attempt attraction or extraction of it, for many have died from this; leaving it inside as long as possible, for it will easily fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days. And then treat the wound if it is visible to you, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20: On the treatment of knots that occur at the site of a fracture.
Many times this knot occurs at the site of healing from a fracture, especially near the joints, and it takes on the shape of the limb; and it easily prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Consider whether the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have styptic properties, such as aloe, alum, myrrh, gum tragacanth, and similar ones; so take some of these or all of them, and mix them with styptic wine or albumen of egg white, or vinegar, and apply this mixture to the swelling on a piece of cloth, and bind it firmly in place.
And leave it bound so that it is not loosened for several days; and then loosen it and reapply a new one until the swelling has subsided; or apply a flat piece of lead to it, since lead has the property of strengthening everything that is inflamed in the limbs.
But if the swelling has become hard and tight and needs to be opened urgently, then incise it at its highest point, remove the excess or prominent part, or cut it with a sharp instrument until it subsides; and take care of the interior so that it heals.
Chapter 21.
On the restoration of fractures when it is healed and the limb should be gradually restored to its normal function using splints.
When a fracture has healed and the limb is gradually restored to its normal function with splints, this should only be done for several reasons: one of which is an excess of loosening of the ligament and its improper healing; or due to excessive tightness of the ligaments preventing nutrients from reaching the limb; or due to excessive application of ointments; or due to inappropriate movement at the wrong time; or due to insufficient strength in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the treatment for this is to feed the patient and make their heart tender until their blood is replenished, administer baths, and provide joy and happiness around them.
And then apply a plaster on the limb so that the plaster attracts nutrients to it, or continue applying a warm compress to it with water until the nutrients flow back and regain their natural shape.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of fractured bones when they have healed crookedly and it is necessary to avoid operating on them.
When a healed limb has an unevenness or prominence in the fractured bone and swelling, which disfigures the shape of the limb, but the limb is still able to perform its natural movements, then one should not believe the words of someone who claims that the limb has been newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish doctors and healers who have done this in our land, and this operation is highly reprehensible, causing great fear, and its mark is fatal.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then it is appropriate to apply warm water with soft herbs such as elder flowers, rose petals, melilot, and similar ones; and make a plaster with softening ingredients such as a good dough; or take a mucilage of marshmallow root and mix it with gum tragacanth or camphor oil, and apply this mixture as a plaster; or use ripe figs, crush them with dove dung, and similar remedies that are called softening consolidants.
And sometimes it is necessary to dissolve it with gentle and constant friction using moist hands, and to promote movement of the limb in all directions at all times.
But if the crookedness has already progressed and become hard and turned into stone, requiring surgery, then it is necessary to cut off what is above it, dissolve the continuation of the bone, and remove the excess swelling from that area with fine incisors, and make an incision in it. The patient should be given ease and care during this time.
Chapter 23.
On Dislocation and Speech.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints from its position, which prevents movement and disfigures the limb, causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is advisable to quickly restore it to its proper position and not delay, as if it is delayed, the location becomes infected and it becomes difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to move or stretch the limb during the infection, as this can cause spasms and painful cramps.
But when this occurs, it is best to perform bloodletting on the patient and let them rest until the infection subsides; meanwhile, apply warm water and oil compresses to the limb, and gently restore it to its proper position with ease.
And take care of the entire limb in accordance with what is appropriate for its recovery, so that the memory of this injury will be healed.
And I have now organized the chapters on dislocation as previously discussed in relation to fractures, from the uppermost part of the body to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two lower jaws can become dislocated if not treated quickly; and this can happen in one of two ways: either they are slightly loosened from their positions and become softened a little, or they become completely dislocated so that the jaw is loose at the joint where saliva flows from the patient's mouth and they cannot close it or speak clearly, and their tongue becomes wrapped up in their words.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its proper position on its own with little cause in most cases.
If the dislocation is complete, then gentle reduction should be administered immediately, and not delayed.
And the assistant should hold the head of the patient, while the doctor inserts his finger of one hand into the socket of the dislocated jaw inside the mouth of the patient, if the dislocation is on one side; or he inserts both fingers together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hand should be placed outside to guide; and he tells the patient to stop moving their jaw and release it from either side; and he guides the jaw back into place until it returns to its proper location.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer evaporation with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy and not delayed, as we have said.
And so when they return and are guided back into place, and the mouth of the patient is closed and not swollen, then place warm cloth compresses with cerote (a type of resin) on those jaw muscles, and do it gently with wax and oil; and then bind it lightly with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, with their head resting between two pillows, to prevent it from tilting to either side; and do not force them to chew any food; but rather have their food be cut into small pieces until the pains are relieved and the jaws are no longer swollen, then let them eat what they want.
And this should be done easily and without difficulty, without applying pressure on the opening of the mouth for food and drink and speaking, until the jaw is healed and no longer swollen.
And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated for some time and not returned to their proper locations, often this results in fevers and constant swelling, and it is easy for the belly of the patient to become distended, and it is easy for them to vomit pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is a dangerous situation; and often the patient dies within 10 days after experiencing such symptoms.
