Chapter 26.
Chapter 26: On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle (likely referring to dislocation of muscles attached to bones, such as shoulder or hip)
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the bony prominence to below (likely referring to the greater tubercle or lesser tubercle of the humerus for shoulder dislocation)
And the second way is when it is dislocated above the muscle, and this occurs late; and it is not dislocated at the back by the shoulder blade, nor is it dislocated at the front by the nerves. (likely referring to cases where the muscle is dislocated in a specific direction)
And most often it is dislocated and protrudes towards the back side below the bony prominence, especially in those with little flesh, it is easily dislocated; but in those with a lot of flesh, it protrudes outwards with difficulty. (likely referring to cases where the muscle is dislocated based on body composition)
And often in some men, a blow or fall causes the muscle to become abscessed with a putrid abscess, and it is consumed because it has been dislocated. (likely referring to cases where injury or trauma causes infection in the dislocated area)
Therefore, it is necessary that you first attend to this before attempting reduction, and then proceed with the treatment of it. (likely referring to the need to address any infection or abscess before attempting reduction)
And the dislocation of it is recognized when it is towards the back side below the bony prominence; so that you may distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal muscle, because there is a clear difference between them; and you will find in the head of the muscle a concavity below the bony prominence near the touch of the head of the muscle, just like a hollow, and the patient cannot move their hand to their ear, nor can they feel it with any movement.
If it is dislocated towards the side or above, then you will find a clear touch of it and it is not hidden. (likely referring to cases where the muscle is dislocated in those directions)
And this dislocation can be easily reduced when it is fresh and the patient is a child. (likely referring to cases where the muscle is dislocated in young patients)
And the reduction of it is done by having the assistant raise the patient's arm above their head, and then placing their fingers below the bony prominence, and raising the joint with strength to its proper location; and the assistant should continue to lift the arm while extending it outwards, and then place it down gently as it returns. (likely referring to a specific technique used for reduction of shoulder dislocation)
But if it does not return as we have said, and the dislocation has just occurred, many days ago; then it is necessary that the patient bathe in warm water and be administered a poultice that softens and loosens it, just like boiled roots of elder, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and this should be applied. (likely referring to the use of warm compresses or poultices to help reduce muscle tension, inflammation and swelling)
And then the patient should be placed lying on their back and a woolen pad should be placed below the bony prominence that is covered in linen cloth that has moderate compression; and the doctor should place his knee on the pad, and lift the head of the muscle with strength, and pull the patient's arm to prevent it from being injured at the back side; and another assistant should hold the head of the patient to prevent injury at the back side, because it will quickly return to its proper location.
And if you wish to reduce it in this manner: have a person who is taller than the patient present and stand on the side of the affected area, then insert their muscle below the bony prominence of the patient and lift the bony prominence upwards until the patient is suspended in air; and another assistant should pull the patient's arm downwards from the lower part of their abdomen. But if the patient is lightweight, then it is better that they are suspended by something else, such as a rope or sling, to add weight and help return the dislocation quickly. (likely referring to using a device or apparatus to assist in reduction)
And it is also reduced in another way: by placing a long rod on the ground with a round top, like a mortar pestle, that is neither thick nor thin; and have the patient sit on it; then place a linen cloth over the top of the rod, and the patient should stand along the length of the rod, and extend their arm downwards on one side, and pull the rest of their body strongly on the other side, so that the joint returns to its proper location quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult for all the reasons we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a rod that is two arm-lengths long, and four fingers wide, and its thickness should be adjusted to fit the bony prominence in this shape:
And place linen cloths over the round top of it, so that the rod does not harm the patient. (likely referring to protecting patient's skin and soft tissues from injury during reduction)
And place it below the bony prominence of the patient and have their arm or hand extended over the rod towards the back side, and bind the rod to the patient's body, arm and hand. (likely referring to securing the rod in place with bandages or straps)
And have the patient's arm placed on a step of a ladder according to its width, and their hand extended towards the back side, and the rest of their body suspended from the other side, because then the joint will quickly return to its proper location. (likely referring to using a ladder or steps to apply traction force and elevation, which would help reduce dislocation)
And when the insertion of it has been completed in any manner that is possible for you, then place a woolen pad below the bony prominence of moderate size; and apply a prepared plaster made from powdered molasses, wax, and egg white over the entire muscle, as if it were rolled over it; and press firmly below the bony prominence with appropriate compression; and wrap the bandage over the plaster; and bind the patient's arm to their neck, leaving it in this position for 7 days and take care not to injure the patient's arm.
And it is necessary that the patient's food intake be moderate until the affected limb has regained strength, because this facilitates healing. (likely referring to restricting patient's diet and caloric intake to prevent additional strain on muscles and joints)
And then the food should be given gradually more, after 7 or 5 days, and gentle exercise can be performed; and when it is firm and not swollen, then it is healed. (likely referring to gradual reintroduction of normal diet and physical activity as part of recovery process)
If the joint is dislocated due to an excess of humidity that happens to it or for another reason, then it is necessary to apply cautery to it in three strokes, as stated in the chapter on cautery.
And if all of this is done and after seven days the ligament is still loose and the joint is not closed, and the bandages and tightness have been applied several times but it is still not closed, and the catheter cannot be removed from that arm because it is too swollen and cannot be raised above the level of the other parts, then you should know that the nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, have either been cut or stretched or made weak, and you should also know that the joint will never be in its proper position again.
In the case of a dislocation, you should place your foot and hand or the parts behind it in the correct position, and then use force and stretching with your hands to return it to its proper place.
And apply another bandage to that one, and take care of it until it is healed.
And if there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement during the healing process, then the patient should take many baths until the heaviness is relieved and they return to their normal state.
Chapter 27.
On the treatment of hip dislocation.
Know that the joint of the hip is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated on all sides, but most commonly in the front part to the back part, and the dislocation of it is not hidden from you because it is noticeable when sitting down or lying down according to the way it is dislocated. And when you adjust the dislocated hip with your hand, this is clearly shown to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend its arm or touch its own muscle.
And it is necessary to act quickly and reduce the dislocation before pus forms, because if pus does form, then reducing the dislocation becomes difficult and healing may not occur at all, especially when it occurs in the back parts, which is the worst of all types of dislocations, causing great pain and often leading to death.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's hand should be extended with two hands, and their arms should be spread out, and the hands of the doctor should be on top of the hip pushing down, while using the pads of the fingers or the edge of the palm to push the joint back into place.
But if the dislocation is in the front, then it should be returned by bending the patient's hand towards their own hand until they strike the hip with the edge of their palm.
And if the dislocation does not respond to reduction, then apply strong force and extension.
And it is necessary that two assistants hold the patient's arms and two others maintain this extension during the stretching, to prevent any rebounding during the process.
And then the arms should be rotated in all directions, as they are wrapped with long folded cloth or bandages over the hand.
And when the doctor touches the joint with their hand, anoint it with some oil to facilitate the movement of the joint and make it easier; and then press down on the joint with strong force until it returns to its proper position.
And after reducing it, a plaster should be applied to the area with styptic and absorbent properties, made from egg white, and extracted with bandages that are not too tight, and the arm should be suspended from the neck and left in this state for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and so if the joint is in its proper position, then release the ligament of it and leave it alone.
And if you see that the joint is not strengthening, then reapply the plaster and ligament, and leave it for several days until it is extracted; and then release it.
But if there is heaviness in the reduction of the joint and its position is unstable, then administer moisture in the form of baths and gentle friction, along with compression, until it is relieved; or place a cushion of soft material under the joint, and bind it, leaving it on the bone day and night. And then apply that (cushion), and put it in a bath; and when it dries, then rub the joint with moderate friction, and then reapply the cushion of soft material, which is round and movable, once, twice, three times. Decay has already spread within the interior of the tooth before it breaks open when it is being cleaned or scraped.
And if you wish, place it (the affected limb) on a moist and scarified cow's split, smeared with butter and stretched over it; and do this several times, because the split is moist and helps to restore its proper position.
Chapter 28.
On the treatment of dislocation of the wrist, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
The wrist of the hand is often dislocated and the reduction of this dislocation is easy, unlike other joints. However, it is still necessary to act quickly in reducing the dislocation at the time when it occurs before it becomes inflamed or infected.
The reduction of the dislocation of the wrist is done by placing the patient's hand on a table and having an assistant extend the arm of the affected individual, while placing their own hand or palm over the back of the wrist joint. Then, gentle pressure is applied to push the joint back into its proper position.[20]
However, it is important to consider whether the palm is facing down towards the patient's belly or up towards the ceiling. If the palm is facing down, then the patient should place the back of their hand on the table for the extension and reduction. On the other hand, if the dislocation is in front of the palm, then the patient's hand should be placed with the palm facing up on the table, allowing the doctor to apply pressure on top of the joint.[23]
And therefore, if it returns to its proper position quickly, that is good. But if not, then strengthen it with a plaster that reduces inflammation and leave it on; and do not repeat this process, because otherwise, the swelling will only increase and make reduction more difficult in the following days.[26]
However, the joint may remain dislocated due to its twisting shape, and the patient may not experience any noticeable symptoms other than weakness in the affected limb. In this case, it is important to recognize that the nerves may be damaged or stretched, and that strengthening the area with cauterization may be necessary to promote healing. This should only be done with caution, however, as excessive heat can cause further damage to the affected tissues.[29]
Therefore, when the dislocation has been reduced, place a plaster on it as we have recommended, and leave it on to heal for five days. Then, release the plaster and allow it to rest, while gradually beginning to exercise the affected limb.
If the patient's movements are impeded and an accident causing gravity befalls him, then gently apply warm water and perform friction several times until he is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of dislocations of the fingers.
Fingers are often dislocated in various places.
And when a finger on the side of the palm or its belly is dislocated, then stretch out the digit and push the dislocation back into place with your thumbs until it returns to normal.
And then support the head of the digit and suspend it on the side where it is dislocated and leave it for two days.
And then suspend it and stretch it out until it becomes straight in its proper position on that day.
And when it is night, bind it with this bandage and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it again at night; and do this for several days until it becomes straight.
And do the same if it is dislocated in the belly of the hand, binding it to that side and performing this first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae in the back.
When there is a complete dislocation of one of the vertebrae in the back or neck and several vertebrae have moved from their places, then at this point it cannot be cured because death is imminent for the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's excretion is expelled involuntarily and they cannot retain it; and often several members of their body or two sides of them or both arms are paralyzed, or one of these; or a vertebra has moved from its place, because many have moved and much movement has occurred in all directions.
Then it has moved to the sides and is called "gelbozitat".
But the treatment for it is that then you should consider if this lumbago has occurred in childhood, because then there is no cure nor can it be healed completely.
But for those who have it from chance or injury or similar causes, the ancients have described methods of cure in a lengthy discourse, the greatest part of which I will abbreviate here so as to avoid excess verbiage and instead provide a more concise explanation that aligns with my intent, which is what I have presented and set forth through various instruments, which I will show and expand upon.
And of those, only the one that occurs in the back can be cured with what I have recommended.
And it is that the patient lies face-down on a hard surface close to a wall and stretches out their body with the instrument placed beneath them so as not to hurt their feet.
And then place a straight rod firmly in the ground at the edge of the bed near the patient's head and another rod on the other side of the bed at their feet.
And let the assistant hold the rods, not being fixed in place; and let another assistant hold the other rod in the same manner.
And then wrap the patient's head with a soft cloth and tie it tightly around their chin; stretch the end of the cloth to the rod that is by the patient's head and tie it there; and then strengthen the other cloth around their hips, knees, and legs, and adjust all the cloth strips and tie them to the other rod that is by the patient's feet.
And then each assistant should stretch the cloth strips to their respective rods and ensure that both rods and the cloth strips are in their correct places where they have been fastened, but not fixed in place as previously mentioned.
And then the doctor places their palms on the vertebra with force until it returns to its place, or they place a board on it and adjust their feet on top of the board until it returns to its place.
But if it does not return through this method, then take a table that is three cubits in length or width and carve out a space in the wall near the patient where the edge of the table can fit. And place the majority of the table over the vertebrae, and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and pulls strongly so that the vertebra is constricted and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open (the patient) with a turning motion using your hand; and that is, place two sticks of wood on the ground beside the patient's head, the length of each stick being one arm's length, and the distance between the two sticks should be the width of a palm, and make a hole in the middle of each stick, and securely fasten each stick to the ground so that they do not move.
And place a round piece of wood in the hole, and it should turn in the holes of the two sticks.
And at the end of it is a hole in which the stick fits, its length being one palm's width so that it can turn, and there are similar holes in the other two sticks as well.
And pull the ligaments that you tightened on the patient's feet in the hole that is beside their head, and the ligaments that you tightened on the patient's thighs in the hole that is beside their feet.
And you should turn each handle and place a minister who twists the handle with his hand, and they insert the vertebra as we have said.
And when the vertebra has returned to its place and the area is cleaned, then it is necessary to apply a dry plaster with egg white, and cotton; and place over the plaster a piece of wooden board with a width of three fingers or thereabouts, and its length should be sufficient to cover the inflamed area, and place it over the affected vertebra.
And tie it with the ligament that we mentioned earlier, and feed the patient a light diet until they are healed.
And if any residual danger remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and clean with frequent administration of the device mentioned earlier, which has been recommended; and a sheet of lead should be applied to the affected area.
And sometimes it happens that danger is present in the lower vertebrae of the back, and this is due to dislocation; and this bone is at risk, so it is important that no pressure is applied to it with this medicine, as even a small amount can lead to death.
Capital XXxi. (This would be the title of the next section or chapter)
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle only become dislocated, and do not have the same symptoms as other joints with small displacement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip can become dislocated in four ways: it can become dislocated towards the inside, or outside, or front, or back.
And most often, it becomes dislocated towards the inside, and less frequently towards the front or back.
And the sign of a dislocation towards the inside is that when you align the healthy leg of the patient with the affected leg, the affected leg appears longer and more prominent than the healthy leg, and the patient cannot bend their knee as much as the healthy leg, and this is the location of an abscess or boil that causes pain when touched, because the head of the thigh bone has moved forward.
And the sign of a dislocation towards the outside is that the symptoms are opposite to those mentioned above.
And the sign of a dislocation towards the front is that the leg appears to be extended according to its normal length, but it cannot be bent due to pain in the knee joint, and if they try to walk, they are unable to move their legs forward; and their urine becomes retained and the area around the bladder becomes inflamed, and their movement should be limited to a horizontal position. [
And the sign of a dislocation towards the back is that the knee does not extend, and cannot be bent further than the bend in the thigh, and the leg appears shorter than the other leg, and the area around the hip joint becomes soft, and the head of the thigh bone has moved backwards.
However, in the treatment of this dislocation, if the dislocation is old and chronic, you should consider whether it has become fixed due to long-standing immobility, and if so, then you can attempt reduction while being mindful of its position; but if not, then it is important that no force is applied during the treatment.
But in the case of a recent dislocation that is one of the four types mentioned earlier, it is necessary to manipulate the joint and extend it towards the inside and outside, while also moving it from side to side, as this will allow it to easily return to its normal position without requiring further treatment.
However, if the joint does not return to its normal position through manipulation alone, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant who will extend the leg downwards at the lower end, or upwards towards the thigh, or with a ligament tied above the knee; and another assistant should extend their hand over the patient's abdomen in such a way as to place their fingers on the hip bone, while pulling on the ligament that is tied around the thigh bone; and then pull strongly on the ligament near the hip joint, while holding the end of the ligament with another assistant.
And this extension involves pulling the affected area towards the groin or towards the back to align it with the spine.
And this extension should be done in one motion, so that the patient is raised from the ground with their body suspended in the air.
And this type of extension is common to all four types of dislocation.
And so if the dislocation returns through the method described, it will be good; but if not, then it is necessary for each type of dislocation to proceed with its own treatment as I have described earlier.
In the treatment of a dislocation towards the inside, it is necessary for the patient to lie on their good side, and then you should place ligaments over the hip joint in the area between the head of the thigh bone and the point just below the knee; and then pull upwards on the ligament starting from the groin area and extending towards the highest point of the pelvic bone. [
And have another strong assistant take hold of the patient's arms and press firmly on the large muscle of the affected hip while extending it outwards forcefully, as this will help the dislocation return to its proper position.
And this type of treatment is easier than the others for reducing this limb.
But if the patient resists and does not follow your instructions with this type of treatment, then it is necessary to tie both of their legs together securely with a ligament at the ankles and knees.
And let each ligament be four fingers wide, and the affected leg should be extended more than the healthy leg by two fingers width.
And have the patient suspended by their head over the end of the table or bench, so that they are lying face down, and make sure there is a distance of two arm's length between them and the ground. [
And order a strong young man to press down on the head of the thigh bone while another strong young man pulls up on the thigh, as this will help the joint return to its proper position quickly.
However, in the treatment of a dislocation towards the outside, it is necessary for the patient to lie on their back with their knee bent as described earlier, and then pull strongly on the ligaments over the affected leg and foot.
And place two sticks, one under the patient's feet and the other behind their head, and place another stick adjusted to the middle of the back so that warm cloths can be wrapped around it, in order to prevent the stick from causing discomfort to the patient, especially during the extension movement.
And have each assistant pull on their respective side, while the doctor uses their hand to guide the reduction according to their skill and expertise.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then it is necessary to slightly extend the leg and place it on a mound as described earlier; and place the palm of your right hand over the affected knee and use your other hand to push downwards on the thigh, while pushing up on the calf with the same hand. [
And if the dislocation is towards the back, then it is not necessary to extend the leg downwards and the patient should be raised off the ground; instead, place the patient on a firm surface as described earlier when discussing how to position the patient when the hip joint is dislocated towards the outside, with their face facing upwards and the ligaments extended as previously described.
And it is necessary to apply pressure with a board on the area where the joint has become dislocated.
And when the reduction is completed as necessary, and you see that the patient's legs are of equal length and the patient can extend their leg without resistance or pain, then you know that the limb has returned to its proper position; and in this case, align the hips and apply a plaster with bandages to hold them in place, taking care not to damage the joint in any way. Then instruct the patient to rest for three or four days.
And then remove the bandages and plaster and compare one leg with the other.
And if you see that the legs are equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation has been successfully reduced and can allow the patient to move freely again.
And if you notice any signs of swelling or inflammation, then reapply the bandages and plaster, and immobilize the joint again, taking care to maintain the correct alignment, and leave it in place for three days.
And then remove the bandages and allow the patient to move slowly after several days, once they have regained strength in the affected limb.
Capital XXXII. (This would be the title of the next section or chapter)
On the treatment of a dislocated knee.
The knee joint can become dislocated in three ways: towards the front, towards the back, and towards the sides (but not towards the front).
And the sign of a dislocated knee is that when you command the patient to bend their leg at the knee, you will know that it is dislocated.
And the treatment for all types of knee dislocations is to have the patient lie down and extend their leg if possible; then place your hand on the back of the knee and hold the patient's foot between your legs, while using both palms to press firmly on the front of the knee and applying steady pressure with your fingers. Another assistant should slowly straighten the patient's leg until the knee returns to its proper position.
And the sign that the reduction is successful is when the patient's leg attaches easily and effortlessly to their thigh.
And then apply bandages to immobilize the knee and hold it in place against the thigh for three or four days; then remove the bandages.
And instruct the patient to avoid walking for several days until they have regained their strength and are fully healed.
But if the reduction is unsuccessful, then apply strong traction with ligaments as described earlier in the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Capital XXXIII. (This would be the title of the next section or chapter)
Of the healing of the dislocation of the ankle. (based on the context, it seems that this text is describing a medical procedure for treating a dislocated ankle)
The ankle can sometimes be dislocated due to a small displacement, and at other times it is dislocated as a result of its normal movement, and such a dislocation can occur either within or outside the joint.
And the sign of ankle dislocation is that sometimes the ankle appears swollen, proceeding towards the part where it is dislocated.
But the healing of the displacement (small dislocation) is easy to reduce, and this involves extending it with ease using one's hands and bandaging it until it returns to its proper position.
But in healing when it is dislocated as a result of its normal movement, it is necessary to lay the patient flat and have a strong and capable assistant stand behind him at the affected side in the middle of his back.
Then place your right hand on the upper part of the leg and your left hand below the leg at the level of the ankle joint.
Then pull the leg towards you with your right hand and apply pressure with both hands for two movements, and extend it using your left hand; and push the heel of the foot towards the thigh with your right hand.
And do this twice as I have described.
Then push the heel of the foot towards the thigh for the third time and place your hand at the ankle joint. (it seems that this step is repeating the same movement described earlier but with one less repetition)
So if it returns to its proper position in one or two movements following this method, and sometimes the foot will be swollen, that is good; but if not, then you should repeat the procedure because it will eventually return to its proper position. (This sentence is summarizing the previous instructions for reducing an ankle dislocation and adding a note about possible swelling)
But if you are not authorized to reduce it (the dislocation) using the method described above, then have the patient lie on his back on the ground, place a firm cushion under his buttocks, and position it between his thighs; then wrap him in a blanket so that he does not feel cold. (This sentence is providing an alternative non-invasive treatment option when reducing a dislocation is not recommended or possible)
And let the assistant of the patient hold his thighs, and let another assistant extend the leg with their hands or using a bandage that surrounds the lower leg; and let each assistant pull on the opposite side of the leg to prevent it from being extended too much, while keeping the cushion between the patient's thighs to stabilize his body and prevent it from being pulled downwards by the leg extension. (This sentence is describing a non-invasive method for stretching the leg in order to reduce a dislocation, where assistants provide support and countertraction)
And let the doctor bandage the dislocation with his hands and another assistant hold the healthy thigh towards the lower end, because the dislocation will return easily (to its proper position). (This sentence is describing a manual reduction technique where the doctor uses their hands to manipulate the affected joint and move it back into place while an assistant holds the other side in order to stabilize the limb)
And when the dislocation returns to its proper position and you are certain that it is healed, then apply a poultice and bandage it firmly, and bind the leg to the thigh using a strong bandage. (This sentence is summarizing the treatment steps for ankle dislocation and providing instructions on how to immobilize the joint after reduction)
And you should be careful and protect the nerves that are located behind the ankle, because a strong bandage on top of them can cause damage or severe pain. (This sentence is providing a cautionary note about potential complications that may arise from applying a bandage too tightly or incorrectly)
And let it remain (bandaged) for two or three days; and if the bandage becomes soft, remove it. (This sentence is providing instructions on how long to leave the bandage in place and when to replace it with a new one)
And unwrap (the bandage) on the third or fourth day and forbid the patient to walk for 40 days. (This sentence is providing instructions on when to remove the bandage and how long the patient should avoid weight-bearing activities in order to promote healing and prevent re-injury) But if he is forced to walk before the recommended time, there is a risk that the dislocation may become unstable and infected, leading to delayed or incomplete healing. (This sentence is emphasizing the importance of complying with medical advice and avoiding weight-bearing activities for the recommended duration)
And if an abscess forms near the affected area, then it is necessary to administer treatment as described in our previous discussion, including incision and drainage, wound care, and medication until the infection resolves. (This sentence is acknowledging that complications can arise during the healing process and describing appropriate medical interventions for treating an abscess or other infection)
Chapter XXXIV. (This heading indicates a new section in the text, likely addressing a different medical condition or treatment approach)
Of the healing of dislocation of the toes. (This sentence is identifying the topic of the current chapter and indicating that it deals with the diagnosis and treatment of toe dislocations)
It is necessary that the dislocated toe be bandaged with little extension without effort; and this is not difficult, it can be done easily. (This sentence is describing a basic treatment approach for reducing a toe dislocation, which involves applying gentle traction to realign the affected joint and immobilizing it using a dressing or bandage)
But if the dislocation is in one of the middle toes of the foot, then it is necessary to lay the patient's foot on a flat surface or table, and keep it in the same position as if he were walking normally. (This sentence is describing a different reduction technique for treating a toe dislocation that involves manipulating the affected joint while the foot is in a weight-bearing position) [*]
And you should stand with your weight on the opposite foot, and place your foot over the joints of the affected toe, applying gentle pressure until it returns to its proper position, and ensuring that it is well bandaged and not swollen. (This sentence is summarizing the steps for reducing a middle toe dislocation and emphasizing the importance of post-reduction care)
And then place a table or board under the patient's foot that spans the entire length of it, with two supports at either end, and secure it firmly with gentle traction for three days; and then unwrap it and allow the patient to rest from walking for several days until it has healed and the reduction is secure. (This sentence is describing a method for immobilizing the toe after reduction and emphasizing the importance of allowing adequate time for healing before resuming normal activities)
Chapter XXXV. (This heading indicates a new section in the text, likely addressing a different medical condition or treatment approach)
Of the types of dislocation caused by injury, fracture, or both. (This sentence is identifying the topic of the current chapter and indicating that it deals with differentiating between various causes of joint injuries and how to diagnose and treat them)
When any of these causes occur and you attempt to treat the injury and restore function, often times this results in death. (This sentence is acknowledging that severe joint injuries can have serious consequences and emphasizing the importance of careful assessment and treatment planning)
And so it is not advisable to be overconfident in treating such injuries, but rather the wise and experienced physician who is compassionate and caring, slow to act, cautious, and humble, should use gentle medications that only occasionally cause abscesses and allow the patient to rely on divine judgement. Only treat those whose salvation from death and relief from suffering is possible, and where there is hope for a cure. (This sentence is emphasizing the importance of caution and humility in treating joint injuries, acknowledging that some cases may be beyond medical intervention and recognizing the limits of one's own abilities as a physician) And so you should make every effort to reduce the dislocation at the onset of the injury, before an abscess forms. (This sentence is summarizing the importance of early intervention and prompt treatment for joint injuries in order to prevent complications such as infection)
And if the affected limb returns to its normal position as desired, then administer treatment that prevents abscess formation and manage the injury with appropriate drying agents as needed. (This sentence is providing instructions for post-reduction care and emphasizing the importance of preventing infection and promoting healing)
If the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and there are separated bone fragments, then you should perform reduction of their position, and carry out the treatment described in previous sections for simple injuries. (This sentence is describing how to treat compound joint injuries with associated bone fractures)
And protect and avoid as much as you can any internal injury in the affected limb, following my advice based on fear of complications; for this is better for your relationship with your Creator, more permanent for your glory, and truer health for your body at the right time. (This sentence is emphasizing the importance of taking precautions to prevent internal injuries during treatment and recognizing the benefits of careful medical care) Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly, may God bless him (This sentence is acknowledging the source of the medical text and expressing gratitude for its wisdom) Indeed, amen (This response confirms the previous statement and concludes the text)
